			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Human overpopulation</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">"Overpopulate" and "Overpopulating" redirect here. For the general concept of overpopulation, see <a href="/wiki/Overpopulation" title="Overpopulation">Overpopulation</a>.</div>

<p><b>Human overpopulation</b> occurs when the <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprint</a> of a human population in a specific geographical location exceeds the <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> of the place occupied by that group. Overpopulation can further be viewed, in a long term perspective, as existing when a population cannot be maintained given the rapid depletion of <a href="/wiki/Non-renewable_resources" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-renewable resources">non-renewable resources</a> or given the degradation of the capacity of the environment to give support to the population.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term <i>human overpopulation</i> refers to the relationship between the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">entire human population</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Environment_(biophysical)" title="Environment (biophysical)">environment</a>: the Earth,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> or to smaller geographical areas such as countries. Overpopulation can result from an increase in <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">births</a>, a decline in <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rates</a>, an increase in <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a>, or an <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">unsustainable</a> <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biome</a> and depletion of resources. It is possible for very sparsely populated areas to be overpopulated if the area has a meagre or non-existent capability to sustain life (e.g. a <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>). Advocates of population moderation cite issues like <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> and risk of <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> as a basis to argue against continuing high human population growth and for <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a>. Scientists suggest that the <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">human impact on the environment</a> as a result of overpopulation, profligate <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a> and proliferation of technology has pushed the planet into a new geological <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(geology)" title="Epoch (geology)">epoch</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Human_population" class="mw-redirect" title="Human population">Human population</a> has been growing continuously since the end of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, around the year 1350,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> although the most significant increase has been in the last 50 years, mainly due to <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine#Modern_medicine" title="History of medicine">medical advancements</a> and increase in agricultural productivity. The rate of population growth has been declining since the 1980s. The United Nations has expressed concern on continued population growth in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-7">[7]</a></sup> Recent research has demonstrated that those concerns are well grounded.<sup id="cite_ref-cliodynamics.ru_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cliodynamics.ru-8">[8]</a></sup> As of March 15, 2017 the world's human population is estimated to be 7.491&#160;<a href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" class="mw-redirect" title="1000000000 (number)">billion</a> by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-USCBcite_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USCBcite-9">[9]</a></sup> and over 7 billion by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-UN7bn_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN7bn-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Most contemporary estimates for the carrying capacity of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> under existing conditions are between 4 billion and 16 billion. Depending on which estimate is used, human overpopulation may or may not have already occurred. Nevertheless, the rapid recent increase in human population is causing some concern. The population is <a href="/wiki/World_population_estimates" title="World population estimates">expected</a> to reach between 8 and 10.5 billion between the years 2040<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN-15">[15]</a></sup> In May 2011, the United Nations increased the medium variant projections to 9.3 billion for 2050 and 10.1 billion for 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The recent rapid increase in human population over the past three centuries has raised concerns that the planet may not be able to sustain present or future numbers of inhabitants. The <a href="/wiki/IAP_statement_on_population_growth" title="IAP statement on population growth">InterAcademy Panel Statement on Population Growth</a>, circa 1994, stated that many environmental problems, such as rising levels of <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric carbon dioxide">atmospheric carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, are aggravated by the population expansion.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Other problems associated with overpopulation include the increased demand for resources such as fresh water and food, <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, consumption of natural resources (such as <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>) faster than the rate of regeneration, and a deterioration in living conditions. Wealthy but highly populated territories like <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a> rely on food imports from overseas.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> This was severely felt during the <a href="/wiki/World_war#The_World_Wars" title="World war">World Wars</a> when, despite food efficiency initiatives like "<a href="/wiki/Dig_for_victory#World_War_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Dig for victory">dig for victory</a>" and <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">food rationing</a>, Britain needed to <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Atlantic" title="Battle of the Atlantic">fight to secure import routes</a>. However, many believe that waste and <a href="/wiki/Over-consumption" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-consumption">over-consumption</a>, especially by wealthy nations, is putting more strain on the environment than overpopulation.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most countries have no direct policy of limiting their birth rates, but the rates have still fallen due to education about family planning and increasing access to birth control and contraception. Only <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> had imposed <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">legal restrictions on having more than one child</a>. <a href="/wiki/Space_colonization" title="Space colonization">Extraterrestrial settlement</a> and other technical solutions have been proposed as ways to mitigate overpopulation in the future.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_concern">History of concern</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History of concern">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City,_Vietnam.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG/275px-Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG" width="275" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG/413px-Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG/550px-Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City%2C_Vietnam.JPG 2x" data-file-width="5184" data-file-height="3456" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Overpopulation_in_H%E1%BB%93_Ch%C3%AD_Minh_City,_Vietnam.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Thousands of scooters make their way through <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City" title="Ho Chi Minh City">Ho Chi Minh City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Concern about overpopulation is an ancient topic. <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> was a resident of the city of <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> in the second century <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, when the population of the world was about 190 million (only 3–4% of what it is today). He notably said: "What most frequently meets our view (and occasions complaint) is our teeming population. Our numbers are burdensome to the world, which can hardly support us.... In very deed, pestilence, and famine, and wars, and earthquakes have to be regarded as a remedy for nations, as the means of pruning the luxuriance of the human race." Before that, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and others broached the topic as well.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Throughout history, population growth has usually been slow despite high <a href="/wiki/Birth_rates" class="mw-redirect" title="Birth rates">birth rates</a>, due to war, <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plagues</a> and other diseases, and high <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a>. During the 750 years before the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, the world's population increased very slowly, remaining under 250 million.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the beginning of the 19th century, the world population had grown to a billion individuals, and intellectuals such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> predicted that mankind would outgrow its available resources, because a finite amount of land would be incapable of supporting a population with a limitless potential for increase.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mercantilism" title="Mercantilism">Mercantillists</a> argued that a large population was a form of wealth, which made it possible to create bigger markets and armies.</p>
<p>During the 19th century, Malthus's work was often interpreted in a way that blamed the poor alone for their condition and helping them was said to worsen conditions in the long run.<sup id="cite_ref-Claeys_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claeys-23">[23]</a></sup> This resulted, for example, in the <a href="/wiki/English_poor_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="English poor laws">English poor laws</a> of 1834<sup id="cite_ref-Claeys_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Claeys-23">[23]</a></sup> and in a hesitating response to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Irish Great Famine</a> of 1845–52.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UN Population Assessment Report of 2004 projects that the world population will plateau by 2050 and will remain stable until 2300.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> A 2014 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Science_(journal)" title="Science (journal)">Science</a></i> challenges this projection, asserting that population growth will continue into the next century.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Adrian Raftery, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a> professor of statistics and sociology and one of the contributors to the study, says: "The consensus over the past 20 years or so was that world population, which is currently around 7 billion, would go up to 9 billion and level off or probably decline. We found there's a 70 percent probability the world population will not stabilize this century. Population, which had sort of fallen off the world's agenda, remains a very important issue."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> A more recent UN projection suggests the population could grow to as many as 15 billion by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-15billion_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15billion-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_population">Human population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Human population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_population_growth">History of population growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History of population growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">World population</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:227px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg/225px-Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg" width="225" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg/338px-Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg/450px-Poulation-since-10000BC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="599" data-file-height="381" /></a>

<p>The human population has gone through a number of periods of growth since the dawn of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> period, around 10,000 BCE. The beginning of civilization roughly coincides with the receding of <a href="/wiki/Glacial_ice" class="mw-redirect" title="Glacial ice">glacial ice</a> following the end of the <a href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It is estimated that between 1–5 million people, subsisting on hunting and <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">foraging</a>, inhabited the Earth in the period before the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>, when human activity shifted away from <a href="/wiki/Hunter_gatherer#History" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunter gatherer">hunter-gathering</a> and towards <a href="/wiki/Oasis_Theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Oasis Theory">very primitive farming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around 8000 BCE, at the <a href="/wiki/Agriculture#History" title="Agriculture">dawn of agriculture</a>, the population of the world was approximately 5 million.<sup id="cite_ref-worldometer_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldometer-33">[33]</a></sup> The next several millennia saw a steady increase in the population, with very rapid growth beginning in 1000 BCE, and a peak of between 200 and 300 million people in 1 BCE.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> caused <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">Europe's population</a> to drop by around 50% between 541 and the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Steady growth resumed in 800 CE.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> However, growth was again disrupted by frequent <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plagues</a>; most notably, the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> during the 14th century. The effects of the Black Death are thought to have reduced the world's population, then at an estimated 450 million, to between 350 and 375 million by 1400.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The population of Europe stood at over 70 million in 1340;<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> these levels did not return until 200 years later.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> England's population reached an estimated 5.6 million in 1650, up from an estimated 2.6 million in 1500.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> New crops from the Americas via the Spanish colonizers in the 16th century contributed to the population growth.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In other parts of the globe, China's population at the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> in 1368 stood close to 60 million, approaching 150 million by the end of the dynasty in 1644.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The population of the Americas in 1500 may have been between 50 and 100 million.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Encounters between European explorers and populations in the rest of the world often introduced local <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">epidemics</a> of extraordinary virulence. Archaeological evidence indicates that the death of around 90% of the <a href="/wiki/Population_history_of_American_indigenous_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Population history of American indigenous peoples">Native American population</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> was caused by <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, measles, and influenza.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Europeans introduced diseases alien to the indigenous people, therefore they did not have immunity to these foreign diseases.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the start of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, during the 18th century, the rate of population growth began to increase. By the end of the century, the world's population was estimated at just under 1 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-censushistorical_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censushistorical-46">[46]</a></sup> At the turn of the 20th century, the world's population was roughly 1.6 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-censushistorical_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-censushistorical-46">[46]</a></sup> By 1940, this figure had increased to 2.3 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>

<p>Dramatic growth beginning in 1950 (above 1.8% per year) coincided with greatly increased food production as a result of the industrialization of agriculture brought about by the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-The_limits_of_a_Green_Revolution_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_limits_of_a_Green_Revolution-49">[49]</a></sup> The rate of human population growth peaked in 1964, at about 2.1% per year.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>'s population grew from 97 million in 1961 to 237.6 million in 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> a 145% increase in 49 years. In <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the population grew from 361.1 million people in 1951 to just over 1.2 billion by 2011,<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> a 235% increase in 60 years.</p>

</table>
<p>There is concern over the sharp population increase in many countries, especially in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, that has occurred over the last several decades, and that it is creating problems with land management, natural resources and access to water supplies.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>The population of <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a> has, for example, grown from 6,279,921 in 1993 to 10,329,208 in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-cia_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cia-57">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">DRC</a> are witnessing a similar growth in population. The situation is most acute in western, central and eastern Africa.<sup id="cite_ref-cliodynamics.ru_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cliodynamics.ru-8">[8]</a></sup> Refugees from places like <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> have further strained the resources of neighboring states like Chad and Egypt. Chad is also host to roughly 255,000 <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a> from Sudan's <a href="/wiki/Darfur" title="Darfur">Darfur</a> region, and about 77,000 refugees from the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>, while approximately 188,000 Chadians have been displaced by their own civil war and famines, have either fled to either the Sudan, the Niger or, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections_of_population_growth">Projections of population growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Projections of population growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Projections_of_population_growth" title="Projections of population growth">Projections of population growth</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">
<tr>
<th>Continent</th>
<th>Projected 2050 population<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Africa</td>
<td>1.8 billion</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Asia</td>
<td>5.3 billion</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Europe</td>
<td>628 million</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Latin America and Caribbean</td>
<td>809 million</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>North America</td>
<td>392 million</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>According to projections, the world population will continue to grow until at least 2050, with the population reaching 9 billion in 2040,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/World_population#Forecasts" title="World population">predictions</a> putting the population as high as 11 billion in 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> By 2100, the population could reach 15 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-15billion_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15billion-29">[29]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Walter_Greiling" title="Walter Greiling">Walter Greiling</a> projected in the 1950s that world population would reach a peak of about nine billion, in the 21st century, and then stop growing, after a readjustment of the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a> and a sanitation of the tropics.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2000, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> estimated that the world's population was growing at the rate of 1.14% (or about 75 million people) per year and according to data from the CIA's <a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">World Factbook</a>, the world human population currently increases by 145 every minute.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the United Nations' World Population Prospects report:<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> is currently growing by approximately 74 million people per year. Current United Nations predictions estimate that the world population will reach 9.0 billion around 2050, assuming a decrease in average <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertility rate">fertility rate</a> from 2.5 down to 2.0.<sup id="cite_ref-UNPop_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNPop-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>Almost all growth will take place in the less developed regions, where today's 5.3 billion population of underdeveloped countries is expected to increase to 7.8 billion in 2050. By contrast, the population of the more developed regions will remain mostly unchanged, at 1.2 billion. An exception is the United States population, which is expected to increase by 44% from 2008 to 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2000–2005, the average world fertility was 2.65 children per woman, about half the level in 1950–1955 (5 children per woman). In the medium variant, global fertility is projected to decline further to 2.05 children per woman.</li>
<li>During 2005–2050, nine countries are expected to account for half of the world's projected population increase: <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, Democratic Republic of the Congo, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, Uganda, United States, Ethiopia, and China, listed according to the size of their contribution to population growth. China would be higher still in this list were it not for its <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one-child policy</a>.</li>
<li>Global life expectancy at birth is expected to continue rising from 65 years in 2000–2005 to 75 years in 2045–2050. In the more developed regions, the projection is to 82 years by 2050. Among the least developed countries, where life expectancy today is just under 50 years, it is expected to increase to 66 years by 2045–2050.</li>
<li>The population of 51 countries or areas is expected to be lower in 2050 than in 2005.</li>
<li>During 2005–2050, the net number of international migrants to more developed regions is projected to be 98 million. Because deaths are projected to exceed births in the more developed regions by 73 million during 2005–2050, population growth in those regions will largely be due to international migration.</li>
<li>In 2000–2005, net migration in 28 countries either prevented <a href="/wiki/Population_decline" title="Population decline">population decline</a> or doubled at least the contribution of natural increase (births minus deaths) to population growth.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">Birth rates</a> are now falling in a small percentage of <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a>, while the actual populations in many <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed countries</a> would fall without immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-UNPop_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UNPop-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urban_growth">Urban growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Urban growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2006megacities.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2006megacities.PNG/350px-2006megacities.PNG" width="350" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2006megacities.PNG/525px-2006megacities.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/2006megacities.PNG/700px-2006megacities.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2006megacities.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Urban areas with at least one million inhabitants in 2006. 3% of the world's population lived in cities in 1800, rising to 47% at the end of the twentieth century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1800 only 3% of the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world's population</a> lived in cities. By the 20th century's close, 47% did so. In 1950 there were 83 cities with populations exceeding one million; but by 2007 this had risen to 468 agglomerations of more than one million.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> If the trend continues, the world's <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">urban population</a> will double every 38 years, according to researchers. The UN forecasts that today's urban population of 3.2 billion will rise to nearly 5 billion by 2030, when three out of five people will live in cities.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>The increase will be most dramatic in the poorest and least-urbanised continents, Asia and Africa. Projections indicate that most urban growth over the next 25 years will be in <a href="/wiki/Developing_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> One billion people, one-seventh of the world's population, or one-third of urban population, now live in <a href="/wiki/Shanty_towns" class="mw-redirect" title="Shanty towns">shanty towns</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> which are seen as "breeding grounds" for social problems such as crime, <a href="/wiki/Drug_addiction" class="mw-redirect" title="Drug addiction">drug addiction</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, alcoholism, poverty and unemployment. In many poor countries, <a href="/wiki/Slums" class="mw-redirect" title="Slums">slums</a> exhibit high rates of disease due to unsanitary conditions, malnutrition, and lack of basic health care.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2000, there were 18 <a href="/wiki/Megacities" class="mw-redirect" title="Megacities">megacities</a>&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Conurbation" title="Conurbation">conurbations</a> such as Tokyo, <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>, Mexico City, <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, São Paulo, London and New York City&#160;– that have populations in excess of 10 million inhabitants. <a href="/wiki/Greater_Tokyo" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Tokyo">Greater Tokyo</a> already has 35 million, more than the entire population of Canada (at 34.1 million).<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the <i>Far Eastern Economic Review</i>, Asia alone will have at least 10 'hypercities' by 2025, that is, cities inhabited by more than 19 million people, including <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a> (24.9 million people), <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> (25 million), <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> (26.5 million), <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a> (27 million) and <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a> (33 million).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a> has grown from 300,000 in 1950 to an estimated 15 million today, and the Nigerian government estimates that city will have expanded to 25 million residents by 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Chinese experts forecast that Chinese cities will contain 800 million people by 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kathmandu_street.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Kathmandu_street.jpg/220px-Kathmandu_street.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Kathmandu_street.jpg/330px-Kathmandu_street.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Kathmandu_street.jpg/440px-Kathmandu_street.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2304" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kathmandu_street.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Street in <a href="/wiki/Kathmandu" title="Kathmandu">Kathmandu</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From a historical perspective, <a href="/wiki/Technological_revolution" title="Technological revolution">technological revolutions</a> have coincided with population explosions. There have been three major technological revolutions – the tool-making revolution, the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">agricultural revolution</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> – all of which allowed humans more access to food, resulting in subsequent population explosions.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> For example, the use of tools, such as bow and arrow, allowed primitive hunters greater access to high energy foods (e.g. animal meat). Similarly, the transition to farming about 10,000 years ago greatly increased the overall food supply, which was used to support more people. Food production further increased with the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> as machinery, <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> were used to increase land under cultivation as well as crop yields.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> In short, similar to <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> that multiply in response to increased food supply, humans have increased their population as soon as food became more abundant as a result of technological innovations.</p>
<p>Significant increases in human population occur whenever the <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rate</a> exceeds the <a href="/wiki/Death_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Death rate">death rate</a> for extended periods of time. Traditionally, the fertility rate is strongly influenced by cultural and social norms that are rather stable and therefore slow to adapt to changes in the social, technological, or environmental conditions. For example, when death rates fell during the 19th and 20th century – as a result of improved sanitation, child immunizations, and other advances in medicine – allowing more newborns to survive, the fertility rate did not adjust downward fast enough, resulting in significant population growth. Prior to these changes, seven out of ten children died before reaching reproductive age, while today about 95% of newborns in industrialized nations reach adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_mining">Agriculture and mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Agriculture and mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span"><b>This section needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small> <small><i>(February 2014)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">Agriculture</a> has been the main factor behind human population growth. This dates back to prehistoric times, when agricultural methods were first developed, and continues to the present day, with fertilizers, agrochemicals, large-scale mechanization, genetic manipulation, and other technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Humans have always <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_natural_resources" title="Exploitation of natural resources">exploited the environment</a> using the easiest, most accessible resources first. The richest farmland was plowed and the richest mineral ore mined first. Overpopulation will demand ever more creative means in which to satisfy these basic human needs.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological_factors">Psychological factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Psychological factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(September 2016)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Human <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> and the cycle of entrenched <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, as well as the rest of the world's reaction to it, are also causative factors. Areas with greater burden of disease and warfare, contrary to popular belief, do not experience less population growth over the long term, but far more over a sustained period as poverty becomes further entrenched<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (September 2016)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> . This is because parents and siblings who have experienced calamitous conditions suffer from a kind of <a href="/wiki/Post_traumatic_stress_syndrome" class="mw-redirect" title="Post traumatic stress syndrome">post traumatic stress syndrome</a> about losing their family members and overcompensate by having "extra" babies. These extra babies and calamities fuel a <a href="/wiki/Vicious_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Vicious cycle">vicious cycle</a>, and only in the small minority of cases does it cease<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (September 2016)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Human_overpopulation#Dubious" title="Talk:Human overpopulation">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (September 2016)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> . As this cycle is compounded over generations, calamities such as disaster or war take on a <a href="/wiki/Multiplier_(economics)" title="Multiplier (economics)">multiplier effect</a>. For example, the <a href="/wiki/AIDS_in_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="AIDS in Africa">AIDS crisis in Africa</a> is said to have killed 30 million to date, yet during the last two decades money and initiatives to lower population growth by contraception have been sidelined in favor of combating <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-84">[84]</a></sup> feeding the population explosion that we see in Africa today. In 1990, this continent's population was roughly 600 million; today it is over 1,050 million, 150 million more than if the HIV/AIDS crisis had never occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extremes">Extremes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Extremes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Population growth rates between 1950 and 2012 range from a 0.5% increase in the case of Bulgaria to a more than 100 fold increase for the United Arab Emirates (from 79,050 to 8.5 million).<sup id="cite_ref-cenidb1950_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cenidb1950-86">[86]</a></sup> Roughly half of all nations have quadrupled their populations since 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-cenidb1950_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cenidb1950-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic_transition">Demographic transition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Demographic transition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">Demographic transition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility" title="Sub-replacement fertility">Sub-replacement fertility</a></div>
<p>The theory of demographic transition held that, after the <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> and <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> increase, <a href="/wiki/Family_size" class="mw-redirect" title="Family size">family sizes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rates</a> decline. However, as new data has become available, it has been observed that after a certain level of development (<a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">HDI</a> equal to 0.86 or higher) the fertility increases again.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This means that both the worry that the theory generated about aging populations and the complacency it bred regarding the future environmental impact of population growth are misguided.</p>
<p>Factors cited in the old theory included such social factors as later ages of marriage, the growing desire of many women in such settings to seek careers outside <a href="/wiki/Child_rearing" class="mw-redirect" title="Child rearing">child rearing</a> and domestic work, and the decreased need of children in industrialized settings. The latter factor stems from the fact that children <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">perform a great deal of work</a> in small-scale agricultural societies, and work less in industrial ones; it has been cited to explain the decline in birth rates in industrializing regions.</p>
<p>Many countries have high population growth rates but lower <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rates</a> because high population growth in the past skewed the age demographic toward a young age, so the population still rises as the more numerous younger generation approaches maturity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="The material near this tag possibly contains original research. (June 2008)">original research?</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>"Demographic entrapment" is a concept developed by Maurice King, Honorary Research Fellow at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Leeds" title="University of Leeds">University of Leeds</a>, who posits that this phenomenon occurs when a country has a population larger than its carrying capacity, no possibility of migration, and exports too little to be able to import food. This will cause starvation. He claims that for example many sub-Saharan nations are or will become stuck in demographic entrapment, instead of having a demographic transition.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>For the world as a whole, the number of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_and_territories_by_fertility_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="List of countries and territories by fertility rate">children born per woman</a> decreased from 5.02 to 2.65 between 1950 and 2005. A breakdown by region is as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Europe – 2.66 to 1.41</li>
<li>North America – 3.47 to 1.99</li>
<li>Oceania – 3.87 to 2.30</li>
<li>Central America – 6.38 to 2.66</li>
<li>South America – 5.75 to 2.49</li>
<li>Asia (excluding Middle East) – 5.85 to 2.43</li>
<li>Middle East &amp; North Africa – 6.99 to 3.37</li>
<li>Sub-Saharan Africa – 6.7 to 5.53</li>
</ul>
<p>Excluding the observed reversal in fertility decrease for high development, the projected world number of children born per woman for 2050 would be around 2.05. Only the Middle East &amp; North Africa (2.09) and Sub-Saharan Africa (2.61) would then have numbers greater than 2.05.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Carrying_capacity">Carrying capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Carrying capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">Carrying capacity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/220px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg" width="220" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/330px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg/440px-Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_%28bad_effects%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2780" data-file-height="2276" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Familiy_Planning_Ethiopia_(bad_effects).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a> placard in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>. It shows some negative effects of having too many children.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Some groups (for example, the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Global_Footprint_Network" title="Global Footprint Network">Global Footprint Network</a>) have stated that the carrying capacity for the human population has been exceeded as measured using the <a href="/wiki/Ecological_Footprint" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecological Footprint">Ecological Footprint</a>. In 2006, <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">WWF</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Living_Planet_Report" title="Living Planet Report">Living Planet Report</a>" stated that in order for all humans to live with the current consumption patterns of Europeans, we would be spending three times more than what the planet can renew.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Humanity as a whole was using, by 2006, 40 percent more than what Earth can regenerate.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Martin_(diplomat)" title="Roger Martin (diplomat)">Roger Martin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Population_Matters" title="Population Matters">Population Matters</a> states the view: "the poor want to get rich, and I want them to get rich," with a later addition, "of course we have to change consumption habits,... but we've also got to stabilise our numbers".<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Another study by the World Wildlife Fund in 2014 found that it would take the equivalent of 1.5 Earths of biocapacity to meet humanity's current levels of consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>But critics question the simplifications and statistical methods used in calculating Ecological Footprints. Therefore, Global Footprint Network and its partner organizations have engaged with national governments and international agencies to test the results – reviews have been produced by France, Germany, the European Commission, Switzerland, Luxembourg, Japan and the United Arab Emirates.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Some point out that a more refined method of assessing Ecological Footprint is to designate sustainable versus non-sustainable categories of consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> However, if yield estimates were adjusted for sustainable levels of production, the yield figures would be lower, and hence the <a href="/wiki/Overshoot_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Overshoot (ecology)">overshoot</a> estimated by the Ecological Footprint method even higher.</p>
<p>Other studies give particular attention to <a href="/wiki/Resource_depletion" title="Resource depletion">resource depletion</a> and increased world affluence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag needs further explanation. (March 2016)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 1994 study titled <i>Food, Land, Population and the U.S. Economy</i>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Pimentel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="David Pimentel (page does not exist)">David Pimentel</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Giampietro&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mario Giampietro (page does not exist)">Mario Giampietro</a> estimated the maximum <a href="/wiki/U.S._population" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. population">U.S. population</a> for a <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable economy</a> at 200 million.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> And in order to achieve a sustainable economy and avert disaster, the United States would have to reduce its population by at least one-third, and <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> would have to be reduced by two-thirds.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many quantitative studies have estimated the world's carrying capacity for humans, that is, a limit to the world population.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> A meta-analysis of 69 such studies suggests a point estimate of the limit to be 7.7 billion people, while lower and upper meta-bounds for current technology are estimated as 0.65 and 98 billion people, respectively. They conclude: "recent predictions of stabilized world population levels for 2050 exceed several of our meta-estimates of a world population limit".<sup id="cite_ref-Van_Den_BerghRietveld2004_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Van_Den_BerghRietveld2004-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_human_overpopulation">Effects of human overpopulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Effects of human overpopulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">Human impact on the environment</a></div>
<p>Humanity's overall impact on the planet is affected by many factors besides raw population. The lifestyle (including overall affluence and resource utilization) and the pollution (including <a href="/wiki/Carbon_footprint" title="Carbon footprint">carbon footprint</a>) are equally important. In 2008, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> stated that the inhabitants of the developed nations of the world consume resources like oil and metals at a rate almost 32 times greater than those of the developing world, who make up the majority of the human population.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTimes_Diamond_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTimes_Diamond-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some problems associated with or exacerbated by human overpopulation and over-consumption are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Inadequate fresh water<sup id="cite_ref-Shiklomanov-11-32_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiklomanov-11-32-106">[106]</a></sup> for <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Effluent" title="Effluent">effluent</a> discharge. Some countries, like <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, use energy-expensive <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalination</a> to solve the problem of water shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></li>
<li>Depletion of natural resources, especially <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption,_1970-2025,_EIA.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png/220px-World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png/330px-World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2e/World_energy_consumption%2C_1970-2025%2C_EIA.png 2x" data-file-width="389" data-file-height="283" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_energy_consumption,_1970-2025,_EIA.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World energy consumption &amp; predictions, 1970–2025.</div>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li>Increased levels of air pollution, <a href="/wiki/Water_pollution" title="Water pollution">water pollution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">soil contamination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Noise_pollution" title="Noise pollution">noise pollution</a>. Once a country has industrialized and become wealthy, a combination of government regulation and technological innovation causes pollution to decline substantially, even as the population continues to grow.<sup id="cite_ref-opinionjournal-Box_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-opinionjournal-Box-110">[110]</a></sup></li>
<li>Deforestation and loss of ecosystems<sup id="cite_ref-wilson_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-111">[111]</a></sup> that valuably contribute to the global atmospheric oxygen and carbon dioxide balance; about eight million hectares of forest are lost each year.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></li>
<li>Changes in atmospheric composition and consequent <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li>
<li>Loss of <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a> and increase in <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Deforestation and desertification can be reversed by adopting property rights, and this policy is successful even while the human population continues to grow.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">Mass species extinctions</a> and contracting <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> from reduced habitat in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> techniques that sometimes are practiced by <a href="/wiki/Shifting_cultivation" title="Shifting cultivation">shifting cultivators</a>, especially in countries with rapidly expanding rural populations; present <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a> rates may be as high as 140,000 <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a> lost per year.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimm_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimm-121">[121]</a></sup> As of February 2011, the <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List</a> lists a total of 801 animal species having gone extinct during recorded human history,<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> although the vast majority of extinctions are thought to be undocumented.<sup id="cite_ref-Pimm_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pimm-121">[121]</a></sup> Biodiversity would continue to grow at an exponential rate if not for human influence.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Sir <a href="/wiki/David_King_(scientist)" class="mw-redirect" title="David King (scientist)">David King</a>, former chief scientific adviser to the UK government, told a parliamentary inquiry: "It is self-evident that the massive growth in the <a href="/wiki/Human_population" class="mw-redirect" title="Human population">human population</a> through the 20th century has had more impact on biodiversity than any other single factor."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Ehrlich, from Stanford, said population growth is one of the main drivers of the Earth's extinction crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yangtze_River_dolphin" class="mw-redirect" title="Yangtze River dolphin">Yangtze River dolphin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_gray_whale" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic gray whale">Atlantic gray whale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_black_rhinoceros" title="Western black rhinoceros">West African black rhino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merriam%27s_elk" title="Merriam's elk">Merriam's elk</a>, <a href="/wiki/California_grizzly_bear" title="California grizzly bear">California grizzly bear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silver_trout" title="Silver trout">silver trout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blue_pike" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue pike">blue pike</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dusky_seaside_sparrow" title="Dusky seaside sparrow">dusky seaside sparrow</a> are all victims of human overpopulation.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Chris_Hedges" title="Chris Hedges">Chris Hedges</a>, 2009<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></cite></div>
<ul>
<li>High infant and child mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> High rates of infant mortality are associated with poverty. Rich countries with high population densities have low rates of infant mortality.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></li>
<li>Intensive <a href="/wiki/Factory_farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Factory farming">factory farming</a> to support large populations. It results in human threats including the evolution and spread of <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistant_bacteria" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibiotic resistant bacteria">antibiotic resistant bacteria</a> diseases, excessive air and water pollution, and new viruses that infect humans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>Increased chance of the emergence of new <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">epidemics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> For many environmental and social reasons, including overcrowded living conditions, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> and inadequate, inaccessible, or non-existent health care, the poor are more likely to be exposed to <a href="/wiki/Infectious_disease#Mortality_from_infectious_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">Starvation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a><sup id="cite_ref-FAO-Italy_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-Italy-132">[132]</a></sup> or poor diet with ill health and diet-deficiency diseases (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Rickets" title="Rickets">rickets</a>). However, rich countries with high population densities do not have famine.<sup id="cite_ref-w1_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w1-133">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li>Poverty coupled with inflation in some regions and a resulting low level of capital formation. Poverty and inflation are aggravated by bad government and bad economic policies. Many countries with high population densities have eliminated absolute poverty and keep their inflation rates very low.<sup id="cite_ref-heritage-Index-Economic_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heritage-Index-Economic-134">[134]</a></sup></li>
<li>Low <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> in countries with fastest growing populations.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></li>
<li>Unhygienic living conditions for many based upon water resource depletion, discharge of raw sewage<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and solid waste disposal. However, this problem can be reduced with the adoption of sewers. For example, after <a href="/wiki/Karachi,_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Karachi, Pakistan">Karachi, Pakistan</a> installed sewers, its infant mortality rate fell substantially.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></li>
<li>Elevated crime rate due to drug cartels and increased theft by people stealing resources to survive.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li>Conflict over scarce resources and crowding, leading to increased levels of warfare.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></li>
<li>Less personal freedom and more restrictive laws. Laws regulate interactions between humans. Law "serves as a primary social mediator of relations between people".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The higher the population density, the more frequent such interactions become, and thus there develops a need for more laws and/or more restrictive laws to regulate these interactions. It was even speculated by <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a> in 1958 that democracy is threatened due to overpopulation, and could give rise to <a href="/wiki/Totalitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Totalitarian">totalitarian</a> style governments.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/David_Attenborough" title="David Attenborough">David Attenborough</a> described the level of human population on the planet as a multiplier of all other environmental problems.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> In 2013, he described humanity as "a plague on the Earth" that needs to be controlled by limiting population growth.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:25em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>Rich western countries are now siphoning up the planet’s resources and destroying its ecosystems at an unprecedented rate. We want to build highways across the Serengeti to get more rare earth minerals for our cellphones. We grab all the fish from the sea, wreck the coral reefs and put carbon dioxide into the atmosphere. We have triggered a major extinction event ... A world population of around a billion would have an overall pro-life effect. This could be supported for many millennia and sustain many more human lives in the long term compared with our current uncontrolled growth and prospect of sudden collapse.</p>
</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">—<a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a>, 2017<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></cite></div>
<p>Many of these problems are explored in the dystopic science fiction film <i><a href="/wiki/Soylent_Green" title="Soylent Green">Soylent Green</a></i>, where an overpopulated Earth suffers from food shortages, depleted resources and poverty and in the documentary "<a href="/wiki/Aftermath_(2010_TV_series)#Population_Overload" title="Aftermath (2010 TV series)">Aftermath: Population Overload</a>".</p>
<p>Some economists, such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sowell" title="Thomas Sowell">Thomas Sowell</a><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Walter_E._Williams" title="Walter E. Williams">Walter E. Williams</a><sup id="cite_ref-w1_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-w1-133">[133]</a></sup> argue that third world poverty and <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> are caused in part by bad government and bad economic policies. Most biologists and sociologists see overpopulation as a serious threat to the quality of human life.<sup id="cite_ref-wilson_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilson-111">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-145">[145]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">deep ecologists</a>, such as the radical thinker and polemicist <a href="/wiki/Pentti_Linkola" title="Pentti Linkola">Pentti Linkola</a>, see human overpopulation as a threat to the entire <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Resources">Resources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Resources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Overpopulation does not depend only on the size or density of the population, but on the ratio of population to available sustainable resources. It also depends on how resources are managed and distributed throughout the population.</p>
<p>The resources to be considered when evaluating whether an ecological niche is overpopulated include <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">clean water</a>, clean air, food, shelter, warmth, and other resources necessary to sustain life. If the quality of human life is addressed, there may be additional resources considered, such as medical care, education, proper <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">sewage treatment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waste_disposal" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste disposal">waste disposal</a> and energy supplies. Overpopulation places competitive stress on the basic life sustaining resources,<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> leading to a diminished quality of life.<sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>Directly related to maintaining the health of the human population is water supply, and it is one of the resources that experience the biggest strain. With the global population at about 7.5 billion, and each human theoretically needing 2 liters of drinking water, there is a demand for 15 billion liters of water each day to meet the minimum requirement for healthy living (United). Weather patterns, elevation, and climate all contribute to uneven distribution of fresh drinking water. Without clean water, good health is not a viable option. Besides drinking, water is used to create sanitary living conditions and is the basis of creating a healthy environment fit to hold human life. In addition to drinking water, water is also used for bathing, washing clothes and dishes, flushing toilets, a variety of cleaning methods, recreation, watering lawns, and farm irrigation.
