Irrigation poses one of the largest problems, because without sufficient water to irrigate crops, the crops die and then there is the problem of food rations and starvation. In addition to water needed for crops and food, there is limited land area dedicated to food production, and not much more that is suitable to be added. Arable land, needed to sustain the growing population, is also a factor because land being under or over cultivated easily upsets the delicate balance of nutrition supply. There are also problems with location of arable land with regard to proximity to countries and relative population (Bashford 240). Access to nutrition is an important limiting factor in population sustainability and growth. No increase in arable land added to the still increasing human population will eventually pose a serious conflict. Only 38% of the land area of the globe is dedicated to agriculture, and there is not room for much more. Although plants produce 54 billion metric tons of carbohydrates per year, when the population is expected to grow to 9 billion by 2050, the plants may not be able to keep up (Biello). Food supply is a primary example of how a resource reacts when its carrying capacity is exceeded. By trying to grow more and more crops off of the same amount of land, the soil becomes exhausted. Because the soil is exhausted, it is then unable to produce the same amount of food as before, and is overall less productive. Therefore, by using resources beyond a sustainable level, the resource become nullified and ineffective, which further increases the disparity between the demand for a resource and the availability of a resource. There must be a shift to provide adequate recovery time to each one of the supplies in demand to support contemporary human lifestyles. <sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG/220px-Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG/330px-Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG/440px-Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yangzhou_-_industrial_area_west_of_Wenfeng_Temple_-_P1130239.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An industrial area, with a power plant, south of <a href="/wiki/Yangzhou" title="Yangzhou">Yangzhou</a>'s downtown, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/w/index.php?title=David_Pimentel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="David Pimentel (page does not exist)">David Pimentel</a> has stated that "With the imbalance growing between population numbers and vital life sustaining resources, humans must actively conserve cropland, freshwater, energy, and biological resources. There is a need to develop renewable energy resources. Humans everywhere must understand that rapid population growth damages the Earth's resources and diminishes human well-being."<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>
<p>These reflect the comments also of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> in their paper <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.usgs.gov/newsroom/article.asp?ID=653">The Future of Planet Earth: Scientific Challenges in the Coming Century</a>. "As the global population continues to grow...people will place greater and greater demands on the resources of our planet, including mineral and <a href="/wiki/Energy_resources" class="mw-redirect" title="Energy resources">energy resources</a>, open space, <a href="/wiki/Water_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Water crisis">water</a>, and plant and animal resources." "Earth's natural wealth: an audit" by <i><a href="/wiki/New_Scientist" title="New Scientist">New Scientist</a></i> magazine states that many of the minerals that we use for a variety of products are in danger of running out in the near future.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs.gov_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs.gov-153">[153]</a></sup> A handful of geologists around the world have calculated the costs of new technologies in terms of the materials they use and the implications of their spreading to the developing world. All agree that the planet's booming population and rising standards of living are set to put unprecedented demands on the materials that only Earth itself can provide.<sup id="cite_ref-usgs.gov_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-usgs.gov-153">[153]</a></sup> Limitations on how much of these materials is available could even mean that some technologies are not worth pursuing long term.... "Virgin stocks of several metals appear inadequate to sustain the modern 'developed world' quality of life for all of Earth's people under contemporary technology".<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the other hand, some <a href="/wiki/Cornucopian" title="Cornucopian">cornucopian</a> researchers, such as <a href="/wiki/Julian_Simon" title="Julian Simon">Julian L. Simon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bj%C3%B8rn_Lomborg" title="Bjørn Lomborg">Bjørn Lomborg</a> believe that resources exist for further population growth. In a 2010 study, they concluded that "there are not (and will never be) too many people for the planet to feed" according to <a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> Some critics warn, this will be at a high cost to the Earth: "the technological optimists are probably correct in claiming that overall world food production can be increased substantially over the next few decades...[however] the environmental cost of what Paul R. and Anne H. Ehrlich describe as 'turning the Earth into a giant human feedlot' could be severe. A large expansion of agriculture to provide growing populations with improved diets is likely to lead to further <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, loss of <a href="/wiki/Species" title="Species">species</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_erosion" title="Soil erosion">soil erosion</a>, and pollution from pesticides and fertilizer runoff as farming intensifies and new land is brought into production."<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Since we are intimately dependent upon the living systems of the Earth,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> some scientists have questioned the wisdom of further expansion.</p>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Ecosystem_Assessment" title="Millennium Ecosystem Assessment">Millennium Ecosystem Assessment</a>, a four-year research effort by 1,360 of the world's prominent scientists commissioned to measure the actual value of natural resources to humans and the world, "The structure of the world's ecosystems changed more rapidly in the second half of the twentieth century than at any time in recorded human history, and virtually all of Earth's ecosystems have now been significantly transformed through human actions."<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> "Ecosystem services, particularly food production, timber and fisheries, are important for employment and economic activity. Intensive use of ecosystems often produces the greatest short-term advantage, but excessive and unsustainable use can lead to losses in the long term. A country could cut its forests and deplete its fisheries, and this would show only as a positive gain to GDP, despite the loss of capital assets. If the full economic value of ecosystems were taken into account in decision-making, their degradation could be significantly slowed down or even reversed."<sup id="cite_ref-ecosystems-.233_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecosystems-.233-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another study by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP) called the <a href="/wiki/Global_Environment_Outlook" title="Global Environment Outlook">Global Environment Outlook</a><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> which involved 1,400 scientists and took five years to prepare comes to similar conclusions. It "found that human consumption had far outstripped available resources. Each person on Earth now requires a third more land to supply his or her needs than the planet can supply."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It faults a failure to "respond to or recognize the magnitude of the challenges facing the people and the environment of the planet... 'The systematic destruction of the Earth's natural and nature-based resources has reached a point where the economic viability of economies is being challenged – and where the bill we hand to our children may prove impossible to pay'... The report's authors say its objective is 'not to present a dark and gloomy scenario, but an urgent call to action'. It warns that tackling the problems may affect the vested interests of powerful groups, and that the environment must be moved to the core of decision-making... '<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (November 2010)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Although all resources, whether mineral or other, are limited on the planet, there is a degree of self-correction whenever a scarcity or high-demand for a particular kind is experienced. For example, in 1990 known reserves of many natural resources were higher, and their prices lower, than in 1970, despite higher demand and higher consumption. Whenever a price spike would occur, the market tended to correct itself whether by substituting an equivalent resource or switching to a new technology.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fresh_water">Fresh water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fresh water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Water crisis">Water crisis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">Fresh water</a> supplies, on which agriculture depends, are running low worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Water_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Water crisis">water crisis</a> is only expected to worsen as the population increases.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
<p>Potential problems with dependence on desalination are reviewed below, however, the majority of the world's freshwater supply is contained in the polar icecaps, and underground river systems accessible through springs and wells.</p>
<p>Fresh water can be obtained from salt water by <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">desalination</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> derives two thirds of its freshwater by desalination. A number of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear powered</a> desalination plants exist;<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stanford-cohen_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-cohen-170">[170]</a></sup> However, the high costs of desalination, especially for poor countries, make impractical the transport of large amounts of desalinated seawater to interiors of large countries.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> The cost of desalination varies; <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> is now desalinating water for a cost of 53 cents per cubic meter,<sup id="cite_ref-ejpress-water_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ejpress-water-172">[172]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> at 49 cents per cubic meter.<sup id="cite_ref-edie-distinct_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edie-distinct-173">[173]</a></sup> In the United States, the cost is 81 cents per cubic meter ($3.06 for 1,000 gallons).<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to a 2004 study by Zhou and Tol, "one needs to lift the water by 2000 m, or transport it over more than 1600&#160;km to get transport costs equal to the desalination costs. Desalinated water is expensive in places that are both somewhat far from the sea and somewhat high, such as <a href="/wiki/Riyadh" title="Riyadh">Riyadh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harare" title="Harare">Harare</a>. In other places, the dominant cost is desalination, not transport. This leads to somewhat lower costs in places like Beijing, <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">Zaragoza</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix</a>, and, of course, coastal cities like <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a>." Thus while the study is generally positive about the technology for affluent areas that are proximate to oceans, it concludes that "Desalinated water may be a solution for some water-stress regions, but not for places that are poor, deep in the interior of a continent, or at high elevation. Unfortunately, that includes some of the places with biggest water problems."<sup id="cite_ref-uni-hamburg.de_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni-hamburg.de-175">[175]</a></sup> Another potential problem with desalination is the byproduction of saline <a href="/wiki/Brine" title="Brine">brine</a>, which can be a major cause of marine pollution when dumped back into the oceans at high temperatures."<sup id="cite_ref-uni-hamburg.de_175-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uni-hamburg.de-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>The world's largest desalination plant is the <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Ali" title="Jebel Ali">Jebel Ali</a> Desalination Plant (Phase 2) in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>, which can produce 300 million cubic metres of water per year,<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> or about 2500 gallons per second. The largest desalination plant in the US is the one at <a href="/wiki/Tampa_Bay" title="Tampa Bay">Tampa Bay</a>, Florida, which began desalinating 25 million gallons (95000 m³) of water per day in December 2007. A 17 January 2008, article in the <i><a href="/wiki/Wall_Street_Journal" class="mw-redirect" title="Wall Street Journal">Wall Street Journal</a></i> states, "Worldwide, 13,080 desalination plants produce more than 12 billion gallons of water a day, according to the International Desalination Association."<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> After being desalinated at <a href="/wiki/Jubail" title="Jubail">Jubail</a>, Saudi Arabia, water is pumped 200 miles (320&#160;km) inland though a pipeline to the capital city of <a href="/wiki/Riyadh" title="Riyadh">Riyadh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, new data originating from the <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Recovery_and_Climate_Experiment" title="Gravity Recovery and Climate Experiment">GRACE</a> experiments and isotopic testing done by the <a href="/wiki/IAEA" class="mw-redirect" title="IAEA">IAEA</a> show that the <a href="/wiki/Nubian_aquifer" class="mw-redirect" title="Nubian aquifer">Nubian aquifer</a>—which is under the largest, driest part of the earth's surface, has enough water in it to provide for "at least several centuries". In addition to this, new and highly detailed maps of the earth's underground reservoirs will be soon created from these technologies that will further allow proper budgeting of cheap water.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some scientists argue that there is enough food to support the world population,<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup> and some dispute this, particularly if sustainability is taken into account.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many countries rely heavily on imports. <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> rely on imports for 40% of their grain supply. <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> import more than 90%. And just 6 countries – <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, Australia, Canada, France, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and the USA – supply 90% of grain exports. In recent decades the US alone supplied almost half of world grain exports.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-The_Food_Bubble_Economy_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Food_Bubble_Economy-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2001 United Nations report says population growth is "the main force driving increases in agricultural demand" but "most recent expert assessments are cautiously optimistic about the ability of global food production to keep up with demand for the foreseeable future (that is to say, until approximately 2030 or 2050)", assuming declining population growth rates.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, the observed figures for 2007 show an actual increase in absolute numbers of undernourished people in the world, 923 million in 2007 versus 832 million in 1995.;<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> the more recent FAO estimates point to an even more dramatic increase, to 1.02 billion in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_perspective">Global perspective</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Global perspective">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">2007–2008 world food price crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/220px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/330px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Food_production_per_capita.svg/440px-Food_production_per_capita.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Food_production_per_capita.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Growth in food production has been greater than population growth.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The amounts of natural resources in this context are not necessarily fixed, and their distribution is not necessarily a <a href="/wiki/Zero-sum_game" title="Zero-sum game">zero-sum game</a>. For example, due to the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> and the fact that more and more land is appropriated each year from wild lands for agricultural purposes, the worldwide production of food had steadily increased up until 1995. World food production per person was considerably higher in 2005 than 1961.</p>
<p>As world population doubled from 3 billion to 6 billion, daily <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calorie</a> consumption in poor countries increased from 1,932 to 2,650, and the percentage of people in those countries who were malnourished fell from 45% to 18%. This suggests that Third World poverty and famine are caused by underdevelopment, not overpopulation.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> However, others question these statistics.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-189">[189]</a></sup> From 1950 to 1984, as the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a> transformed agriculture around the world, grain production increased by over 250%.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> The world population has grown by about four billion since the beginning of the Green Revolution and most believe that, without the Revolution, there would be greater <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> than the UN presently documents.<sup id="cite_ref-The_limits_of_a_Green_Revolution_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_limits_of_a_Green_Revolution-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>The number of <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">people who are overweight</a> has surpassed the number who are undernourished. In a 2006 news story, <a href="/wiki/MSNBC" title="MSNBC">MSNBC</a> reported, "There are an estimated 800 million undernourished people and more than a billion considered overweight worldwide." The U.S. has one of the highest rates of obesity in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> However, studies show that wealthy and educated people are far likelier to eat healthy food,<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> indicating obesity is a disease related to poverty and lack of education and excessive advertising of unhealthy eatables at cheaper cost, high in calories, with little nutritive value are consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG/220px-Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG" width="220" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG/330px-Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG/440px-Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_population_undernourished_world_map.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Percentage of population suffering from malnutrition by country, according to United Nations statistics.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> of the United Nations states in its report <i>The State of Food Insecurity in the World 2006</i>, that while the number of undernourished people in the developing countries has declined by about three million, a smaller proportion of the populations of developing countries is undernourished today than in 1990–92: 17% against 20%. Furthermore, FAO's projections suggest that the proportion of hungry people in developing countries could be halved from 1990–92 levels to 10% by 2015. The FAO also states "We have emphasized first and foremost that reducing hunger is no longer a question of means in the hands of the global community. The world is richer today than it was ten years ago. There is more food available and still more could be produced without excessive upward pressure on prices. The knowledge and resources to reduce hunger are there. What is lacking is sufficient political will to mobilize those resources to the benefit of the hungry."<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2008, the price of grain has increased due to more farming used in <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> world <a href="/wiki/Oil_prices" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil prices">oil prices</a> at over $100 a barrel,<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> global <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> loss of agricultural land to residential and industrial development,<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> and growing consumer demand in China and India<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">Food riots</a> have recently taken place in many countries across the world.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> An epidemic of stem rust on wheat caused by race <a href="/wiki/Ug99" title="Ug99">Ug99</a> is currently spreading across Africa and into Asia and is causing major concern. A virulent wheat disease could destroy most of the world's main wheat crops, leaving millions to starve. The fungus has spread from Africa to <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, and may already be in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NewSci_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewSci-209">[209]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">Food security</a> will become more difficult to achieve as resources run out. Resources in danger of becoming depleted include <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peak_phosphorus" title="Peak phosphorus">phosphorus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peak_grain" class="mw-redirect" title="Peak grain">grain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">fish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peak_water" title="Peak water">water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> The British scientist <a href="/wiki/John_Beddington" title="John Beddington">John Beddington</a> predicted in 2009 that supplies of energy, food, and water will need to be increased by 50% to reach demand levels of 2030.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO), food supplies will need to be increased by 70% by 2050 to meet projected demands.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Africa">Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <i>Population Reference Bureau</i> in the US reported that the population of Sub-Saharan Africa - the poorest region in the continent - is rising faster than most of the rest of the world, and that "Rapid population growth makes it difficult for economies to create enough jobs to lift large numbers of people out of poverty." Seven of the 10 countries in Sub-Saharan Africa with the highest fertility rates also appear among the bottom 10 listed on the United Nations' Human Development Index.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">Hunger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a> kill nearly 6 million children a year, and more people are malnourished in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> this decade than in the 1990s, according to a report released by the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>. In sub-Saharan Africa, the number of malnourished people grew to 203.5 million people in 2000–02 from 170.4 million 10 years earlier says <i>The State of Food Insecurity in the World</i> report. In 2001, 46.4% of people in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> were living in extreme poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg/220px-Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg" width="220" height="324" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg/330px-Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg/440px-Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg 2x" data-file-width="869" data-file-height="1280" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhaka_street_crowds.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a> street crowds. <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>According to a 2004 article from the BBC, China, the world's most populous country, suffers from an "<a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> surge". The article stated that, "Altogether, around 200 million people are thought to be overweight, 22.8% of the population, and 60 million (7.1%) obese".<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> More recent data indicate China's grain production peaked in the mid-1990s, due to increased extraction of <a href="/wiki/Groundwater" title="Groundwater">groundwater</a> in the North China plain.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_countries">Other countries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Other countries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Japan may face a food crisis that could reduce daily diets to the austere meals of the 1950s, believes a senior government adviser.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_as_a_function_of_food_availability">Population as a function of food availability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Population as a function of food availability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Thinkers from a wide range of academic fields and political backgrounds—including agricultural scientist David Pimentel,<sup id="cite_ref-Hopfenberg_and_Pimentel_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hopfenberg_and_Pimentel-221">[221]</a></sup> behavioral scientist Russell Hopfenberg,<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> right-wing anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Abernethy" title="Virginia Abernethy">Virginia Abernethy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> ecologist <a href="/wiki/Garrett_Hardin" title="Garrett Hardin">Garrett Hardin</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> ecologist and anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Peter_Farb" title="Peter Farb">Peter Farb</a>, journalist <a href="/wiki/Richard_Manning" title="Richard Manning">Richard Manning</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> environmental biologist Alan D. Thornhill,<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> cultural critic and writer <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Quinn" title="Daniel Quinn">Daniel Quinn</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-primitivist" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarcho-primitivist">anarcho-primitivist</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Zerzan" title="John Zerzan">John Zerzan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup>—propose that, like all other animal populations, human populations predictably grow and shrink according to their available food supply, growing during an abundance of food and shrinking in times of scarcity.</p>
<p>Proponents of this theory argue that every time food production is increased, the population grows. Most human populations throughout history validate this theory, as does the overall current global population. Populations of <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a> fluctuate in accordance with the amount of available food. The world human population began increasing after the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> and its increased food supply. This was, subsequent to the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>, followed by even more severely accelerated population growth, which continues today. Often, wealthier countries send their surplus food resources to the aid of starving communities; however, proponents of this theory argue that this seemingly beneficial notion only results in further harm to those communities in the long run. Peter Farb, for example, has commented on the paradox that "intensification of production to feed an increased population leads to a still greater increase in population."<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> Daniel Quinn has also focused on this phenomenon, which he calls the "<a href="/wiki/Food_Race" title="Food Race">Food Race</a>" (comparable, in terms of both escalation and potential catastrophe, to the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_arms_race" title="Nuclear arms race">nuclear arms race</a>).</p>
<p>Critics of this theory point out that, in the modern era, birth rates are lowest in the <a href="/wiki/Developed_nation" class="mw-redirect" title="Developed nation">developed nations</a>, which also have the highest access to food. In fact, some developed countries have both a diminishing population and an abundant food supply. The United Nations projects that the population of 51 countries or areas, including Germany, Italy, Japan, and most of the states of the former Soviet Union, is expected to be lower in 2050 than in 2005.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This shows that, limited to the scope of the population living within a single given political boundary, particular human populations do not always grow to match the available food supply. However, the global population as a whole still grows in accordance with the total food supply and many of these wealthier countries are major <i>exporters</i> of food to poorer populations, so that, "it is through exports from food-rich to food-poor areas (Allaby, 1984; Pimentel et al., 1999) that the population growth in these food-poor areas is further fueled."<sup id="cite_ref-Hopfenberg_and_Pimentel_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hopfenberg_and_Pimentel-221">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regardless of criticisms against the theory that population is a function of food availability, the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">human population</a> is, on the global scale, undeniably increasing,<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> as is the net quantity of human food produced — a pattern that has been true for roughly 10,000 years, since the human development of agriculture. The fact that some affluent countries demonstrate negative population growth fails to discredit the theory as whole, since the world has become a <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalized system</a> with food moving across national borders from areas of abundance to areas of scarcity. Hopfenberg and Pimentel's findings support both this<sup id="cite_ref-Hopfenberg_and_Pimentel_221-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hopfenberg_and_Pimentel-221">[221]</a></sup> and Quinn's direct accusation that "First World farmers are fueling the Third World population explosion."<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup> Additionally, the hypothesis is not so simplistic as to be rejected by any single case study, as in Germany's recent population trends; clearly other factors are at work to limit the population in wealthier areas: <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive" class="mw-redirect" title="Contraceptive">contraceptive</a> access, educational programs, cultural norms and, most influentially, differing economic realities from nation to nation.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_result_of_water_deficits">As a result of water deficits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: As a result of water deficits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_deficits" class="mw-redirect" title="Water deficits">Water deficits</a>, which are already spurring heavy grain imports in numerous smaller countries, may soon do the same in larger countries, such as China or India, if technology is not used.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> The water tables are falling in scores of countries (including Northern China, the US, and India) owing to widespread <a href="/wiki/Overdrafting" title="Overdrafting">overdrafting</a> beyond <a href="/wiki/Sustainable" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable">sustainable</a> yields.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other countries affected include Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, and Mexico. This overdrafting is already leading to <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">water scarcity</a> and cutbacks in grain harvest. Even with the overpumping of its <a href="/wiki/Aquifers" class="mw-redirect" title="Aquifers">aquifers</a>, China has developed a grain deficit. This effect has contributed in driving grain prices upward. Most of the 3 billion people projected to be added worldwide by mid-century will be born in countries already experiencing <a href="/wiki/Water_shortages" class="mw-redirect" title="Water shortages">water shortages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Desalination" title="Desalination">Desalination</a> is also considered a viable and effective solution to the problem of water shortages.<sup id="cite_ref-ejpress-water_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ejpress-water-172">[172]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-edie-distinct_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-edie-distinct-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>After China and India, there is a second tier of smaller countries with large water deficits – <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, Egypt, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, Mexico, and Pakistan. Four of these already import a large share of their grain. Only Pakistan remains self-sufficient. But with a population expanding by 4 million a year, it will also soon turn to the world market for grain.<sup id="cite_ref-The_Food_Bubble_Economy_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Food_Bubble_Economy-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Land">Land</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Land">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landusepiechart.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Landusepiechart.png/250px-Landusepiechart.png" width="250" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Landusepiechart.png/375px-Landusepiechart.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Landusepiechart.png/500px-Landusepiechart.png 2x" data-file-width="537" data-file-height="501" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landusepiechart.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Percentages of the Earth's surface covered by water, dedicated to agriculture, under conversion, intact, and used for human habitation. While humans ourselves occupy only 0.05% of the Earth's total area, our effects are felt on over one-quarter of the land.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The World Resources Institute states that "Agricultural conversion to croplands and managed <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pastures</a> has affected some 3.3 billion [hectares] – roughly 26 percent of the land area. All totaled, agriculture has displaced one-third of <a href="/wiki/Temperate_forest" title="Temperate forest">temperate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical forest">tropical forests</a> and one-quarter of natural grasslands."<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> Forty percent of the land area is under conversion and fragmented; less than one quarter, primarily in the Arctic and the deserts, remains intact.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> Usable land may become less useful through <a href="/wiki/Salinization" class="mw-redirect" title="Salinization">salinization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a>. <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> may cause flooding of many of the most productive agricultural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> The development of energy sources may also require large areas, for example, the building of <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_dams" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroelectric dams">hydroelectric dams</a>. Thus, available useful land may become a limiting factor. By most estimates, at least half of cultivable land is already being farmed, and there are concerns that the remaining reserves are greatly overestimated.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup></p>
<p>High <a href="/wiki/Crop_yield" title="Crop yield">crop yield</a> vegetables like potatoes and lettuce<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> use less space on inedible plant parts, like stalks, husks, vines, and inedible leaves. New varieties of selectively bred and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrid</a> plants have larger edible parts (fruit, vegetable, grain) and smaller inedible parts; however, many of these gain of agricultural technology are now historic, and new advances are more difficult to achieve. With new technologies, it is possible to grow crops on some marginal land under certain conditions. <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">Aquaculture</a> could theoretically increase available area. <a href="/wiki/Hydroponics" title="Hydroponics">Hydroponics</a> and food from bacteria and fungi, like <a href="/wiki/Quorn_(food_product)" class="mw-redirect" title="Quorn (food product)">quorn</a>, may allow the growing of food without having to consider land quality, climate, or even available sunlight, although such a process may be very energy-intensive. Some argue that not all arable land will remain productive if used for agriculture because some <a href="/wiki/Marginal_land" title="Marginal land">marginal land</a> can only be made to produce food by unsustainable practices like <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> agriculture. Even with the modern techniques of agriculture, the sustainability of production is in question.</p>
<p>Some countries, such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a> and particularly the Emirate of <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a> have constructed large artificial islands, or have created large dam and dike systems, like the Netherlands, which <a href="/wiki/Land_reclamation" title="Land reclamation">reclaim land</a> from the sea to increase their total land area.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> Some scientists have said that in the future, densely populated cities will use <a href="/wiki/Vertical_farming" title="Vertical farming">vertical farming</a> to grow food inside skyscrapers.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-nyc_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-nyc-240">[240]</a></sup> The notion that space is limited has been decried by skeptics, who point out that the Earth's population of roughly 6.8 billion people could comfortably be housed an area comparable in size to the state of Texas, in the United States (about 269,000 square miles or 696,706.80 square kilometres).<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> However, the impact of humanity extends over a far greater area than that required simply for housing.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fossil_fuels">Fossil fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fossil fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">Peak oil</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/220px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/330px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg/440px-Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hubbert_peak_oil_plot.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/M._King_Hubbert" title="M. King Hubbert">M. King Hubbert</a>'s prediction of world petroleum production rates. Modern agriculture is totally reliant on petroleum energy.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Population optimists have been criticized for failing to take into account the depletion of the petroleum required for the production of <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> and fuel for transportation, as well as other <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup> In his 1992 book <i><a href="/wiki/Earth_in_the_Balance" title="Earth in the Balance">Earth in the Balance</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a> wrote, "... it ought to be possible to establish a coordinated global program to accomplish the strategic goal of completely eliminating the internal combustion engine over, say, a twenty-five-year period..."<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> Approximately half of the oil produced in the United States is refined into gasoline for use in internal combustion engines.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup></p>
<p>The report <i>Peaking of World Oil Production: Impacts, Mitigation, and Risk Management</i>, commonly referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hirsch_report" title="Hirsch report">Hirsch report</a>, was created by request for the <a href="/wiki/US_Department_of_Energy" class="mw-redirect" title="US Department of Energy">US Department of Energy</a> and published in February 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-hirsch_report_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hirsch_report-245">[245]</a></sup> Some information was updated in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> It examined the time frame for the occurrence of <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>, the necessary mitigating actions, and the likely impacts based on the timeliness of those actions. It concludes that world oil peaking is going to happen, and will likely be abrupt. Initiating a mitigation crash program 20 years before peaking appears to offer the possibility of avoiding a world liquid fuels shortfall for the forecast period.</p>
<p>Optimists counter that fossil fuels will be sufficient until the development and implementation of suitable replacement technologies—such as nuclear power or various sources of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>—occurs.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup> Methods of manufacturing fertilizers from garbage, <a href="/wiki/Sewage" title="Sewage">sewage</a>, and agricultural waste by using <a href="/wiki/Thermal_depolymerization" title="Thermal depolymerization">thermal depolymerization</a> have been discovered.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wealth_and_poverty">Wealth and poverty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Wealth and poverty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">Demographic transition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Income_and_fertility" title="Income and fertility">Income and fertility</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_living_on_less_than_$1_per_day_1981-2001.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Percentage_living_on_less_than_%241_per_day_1981-2001.png/220px-Percentage_living_on_less_than_%241_per_day_1981-2001.png" width="220" height="197" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Percentage_living_on_less_than_%241_per_day_1981-2001.png/330px-Percentage_living_on_less_than_%241_per_day_1981-2001.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Percentage_living_on_less_than_%241_per_day_1981-2001.png/440px-Percentage_living_on_less_than_%241_per_day_1981-2001.png 2x" data-file-width="733" data-file-height="655" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Percentage_living_on_less_than_$1_per_day_1981-2001.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
As the world's population has grown, the percentage of the world's population living on less than $1 per day (adjusted for inflation) has halved in 20 years. The graph shows the 1981–2001 period.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The United Nations indicates that about 850 million people are <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnourished</a> or <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starving</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FAO-Italy_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-Italy-132">[132]</a></sup> and 1.1 billion people <a href="/wiki/Water_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Water crisis">do not have access</a> to safe <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shiklomanov-11-32_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shiklomanov-11-32-106">[106]</a></sup> Since 1980, the global economy has grown by 380 percent, but the number of people living on less than 5 US dollars a day increased by more than 1.1 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup></p>
<p>The UN <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Report" title="Human Development Report">Human Development Report</a> of 1997 states: "During the last 15–20 years, more than 100 developing countries, and several Eastern European countries, have suffered from disastrous growth failures. The reductions in <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> have been deeper and more long-lasting than what was seen in the industrialised countries during the <a href="/wiki/Great_depression" class="mw-redirect" title="Great depression">depression in the 1930s</a>. As a result, the income for more than one billion people has fallen below the level that was reached 10, 20 or 30 years ago". Similarly, although the proportion of "starving" people in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a> has decreased, the absolute number of starving people has increased due to population growth. The percentage dropped from 38% in 1970 to 33% in 1996 and was expected to be 30% by 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-189">[189]</a></sup> But the region's population roughly doubled between 1970 and 1996. To keep the numbers of starving constant, the percentage would have dropped by more than half.<sup id="cite_ref-ecosystems-.233_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ecosystems-.233-161">[161]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png/400px-TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png" width="400" height="339" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png/600px-TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png/800px-TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png 2x" data-file-width="2583" data-file-height="2188" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TFR_vs_PPP_2015.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graph of <a href="/wiki/Total_Fertility_Rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Total Fertility Rate">Total Fertility Rate</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP (PPP) per capita of the corresponding country</a>, 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As of 2004, there were 108 countries in the world with more than five million people. All of these in which women have, on the average, more than 4 children in their lifetime, have a per capita GDP of less than $5000. Only in two countries with per capita GDP above ~$15,000 do women have, on the average, more than 2 children in their lifetime: these are Israel and Saudi Arabia, with average lifetime births per woman between 2 and 4.</p>
<p>As their income increases, women are <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_liberation" class="mw-redirect" title="Women's liberation">liberated</a> and tend to have fewer "quantity kids", as in two in place of six.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup></p>
<p>The correlation does not imply cause and effect, and can be linked to the interplay of birth rates, death rates and economic development.</p>
<p>Poor living conditions can also cause a very bad effect on the population; diseases such as malaria and HIV/AIDS can also contribute to this. Lack of nutrients, poor sanitation and poor health institutions. Death rate and birth rate can also have a negative effect on the population.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">You know, when we first set up WWF, our objective was to save endangered species from extinction. But we have failed completely; we haven’t managed to save a single one. If only we had put all that money into condoms, we might have done some good. <small><a href="/wiki/Sir_Peter_Scott" class="mw-redirect" title="Sir Peter Scott">Sir Peter Scott</a>, Founder of the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a>.</small><sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup></td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Overpopulation has substantially adversely impacted the environment of Earth starting at least as early as the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-Nielsen_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen-145">[145]</a></sup> According to the Global Footprint Network, "today humanity uses the equivalent of 1.5 planets to provide the resources we use and absorb our waste".<sup id="cite_ref-HO_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HO-256">[256]</a></sup> There are also economic consequences of this <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> attrition.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> Beyond the scientifically verifiable harm to the environment, some assert the moral right of other species to simply exist rather than become extinct. Environmental author <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Rifkin" title="Jeremy Rifkin">Jeremy Rifkin</a> has said that "our burgeoning population and urban way of life have been purchased at the expense of vast ecosystems and habitats. ... It's no accident that as we celebrate the urbanization of the world, we are quickly approaching another historic watershed: the disappearance of the wild."<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup></p>
<p>Says Peter Raven, former President of the <a href="/wiki/American_Association_for_the_Advancement_of_Science" title="American Association for the Advancement of Science">American Association for the Advancement of Science</a> (AAAS) in their seminal work <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://atlas.aaas.org/">AAAS Atlas of Population &amp; Environment</a>, "Where do we stand in our efforts to achieve a sustainable world? Clearly, the past half century has been a traumatic one, as the collective impact of human numbers, affluence (consumption per individual) and our choices of technology continue to exploit rapidly an increasing proportion of the world's resources at an unsustainable rate. ... During a remarkably short period of time, we have lost a quarter of the world's <a href="/wiki/Topsoil" title="Topsoil">topsoil</a> and a fifth of its agricultural land, altered the composition of the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a> profoundly, and destroyed a major proportion of our forests and other natural <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> without replacing them. Worst of all, we have driven the rate of biological <a href="/wiki/Extinction" title="Extinction">extinction</a>, the permanent loss of species, up several hundred times beyond its historical levels, and are threatened with the loss of a majority of all species by the end of the 21st century."</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG/220px-Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG/330px-Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG/440px-Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2554" data-file-height="1915" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Traffic_jam_on_Phu_Nhuan_district.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Traffic congestion in <a href="/wiki/Ho_Chi_Minh_City" title="Ho Chi Minh City">Ho Chi Minh City</a>, Vietnam</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Further, even in countries which have both large population growth and major ecological problems, it is not necessarily true that curbing the population growth will make a major contribution towards resolving all environmental problems.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_World_Population_Report_2001_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_World_Population_Report_2001-259">[259]</a></sup> However, as developing countries with high populations become more industrialized, pollution and consumption will invariably increase.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Worldwatch_Institute" title="Worldwatch Institute">Worldwatch Institute</a> said in 2006 that the booming economies of <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">China</a> and India are "planetary powers that are shaping the global biosphere". The report states:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The world's ecological capacity is simply insufficient to satisfy the ambitions of China, India, Japan, Europe and the United States as well as the aspirations of the rest of the world in a sustainable way.<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[260]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>It is said that if China and India were to consume as much resources per capita as the United States, in 2030 they would each require a full planet Earth to meet their needs.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[261]</a></sup> In the long term these effects can lead to increased conflict over dwindling resources<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> and in the worst case a <a href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian catastrophe</a>.</p>
<p>Many studies link population growth with <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">emissions</a> and the effect of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[263]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[264]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare_and_conflict">Warfare and conflict</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Warfare and conflict">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">excessive growth may reduce output per worker, repress levels of living for the masses and engender strife. <small><a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> (551 – 479 BC)</small><sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup></td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Overpopulation in various countries has become a serious threat to the health of people and a grave obstacle to any attempt to organize peace on this planet. <small><a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> – physicist 1879 – 1955</small><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup></td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>It has been suggested<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[267]</a></sup> that overpopulation leads to increased levels of tensions both between and within countries. Modern usage of the term <a href="/wiki/Lebensraum#Modern_usage" title="Lebensraum">"lebensraum"</a> supports the idea that overpopulation may promote warfare through fear of resource scarcity and increasing numbers of youth lacking the opportunity to engage in peaceful employment (the <a href="/wiki/War#Youth_bulge" title="War">youth bulge theory</a>).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_this_hypothesis">Criticism of this hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Criticism of this hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothesis</a> that population pressure causes increased <a href="/wiki/Warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Warfare">warfare</a> has been recently criticized on the empirical grounds. Both studies focusing on specific historical societies and analyses of cross-cultural data have failed to find positive correlation between population density and incidence of warfare. <a href="/wiki/Andrey_Korotayev" title="Andrey Korotayev">Andrey Korotayev</a>, in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Peter_Turchin" title="Peter Turchin">Peter Turchin</a>, has shown that such negative results do not falsify the population-warfare hypothesis.<sup id="cite_ref-t1_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-t1-268">[268]</a></sup></p>
<p>Population and warfare are dynamical variables, and if their interaction causes sustained oscillations, then we do not in general expect to find strong correlation between the two variables measured at the same time (that is, unlagged). Korotayev and Turchin have explored mathematically what the dynamical patterns of interaction between population and warfare (focusing on internal warfare) might be in both stateless and state societies. Next, they have tested the model predictions in several empirical case studies: early modern England, <a href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Han Dynasty">Han</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang</a> China, and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. Their empirical results have supported the population-warfare theory: that there is a tendency for population numbers and internal warfare intensity to oscillate with the same period but shifted in phase (with warfare peaks following population peaks).</p>
<p>Furthermore, they have demonstrated that in the agrarian societies the rates of change of the two variables behave precisely as predicted by the theory: population rate of change is negatively affected by warfare intensity, while warfare rate of change is positively affected by population density.<sup id="cite_ref-t1_268-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-t1-268">[268]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_Solutions_and_Mitigation_measures">Proposed Solutions and Mitigation measures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Proposed Solutions and Mitigation measures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="cquote" role="presentation" style="margin:auto; border-collapse: collapse; border: none; background-color: transparent; width: auto;">
<tr>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: top; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: left; padding: 10px 10px;">“</td>
<td style="vertical-align: top; border: none; padding: 4px 10px;">Given the current levels of violence by this culture against both humans and the natural world, however, it's not possible to speak of reductions in population and consumption that do not involve violence and privation, not because the reductions themselves would necessarily involve violence, but because violence and privation have become the default of our culture. <small><a href="/wiki/Derrick_Jensen" title="Derrick Jensen">Derrick Jensen</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Endgame_(Derrick_Jensen_books)" title="Endgame (Derrick Jensen books)">Endgame</a></i>, 2006</small><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup></td>
<td style="width: 20px; vertical-align: bottom; border: none; color: #B2B7F2; font-size: 40px; font-family: 'Times New Roman', Times, serif; font-weight: bold; line-height: .6em; text-align: right; padding: 10px 10px;">”</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>There are several solutions and mitigation measures that have the potential to reduce overpopulation. Some solutions are to be applied on a global planetary level (e.g., via <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a> resolutions), some to be applied on a local government, state, city, or other organization level, and some are to be applied on a family or an individual level. Some of the mitigations are ways to implement social norms. Overpopulation is an issue that threatens the state of the environment and therefore societies must make a change in order to reverse some of the environmental effects brought on by current social norms.</p>
<p>For example, in societies like China, the government has put policies in place that regulate the number of children allowed to a couple. Other societies have already begun to implement social marketing strategies in order to educate the public on overpopulation effects. "The intervention can be widespread and done at a low cost. A variety of print materials (flyers, brochures, fact sheets, stickers) needs to be produced and distributed throughout the communities such as at local places of worship, sporting events, local food markets, schools and at car parks (taxis / bus stands)."<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup></p>
<p>Such prompts work to introduce the problem so that social norms are easier to implement. Certain government policies are making it easier and more socially acceptable to use contraception and abortion methods. An example of a country whose laws and norms are hindering the global effort to slow population growth is Afghanistan. "The approval by Afghan President Hamid Karzai of the Shia Personal Status Law in March 2009 effectively destroyed Shia women's rights and freedoms in Afghanistan. Under this law, women have no right to deny their husbands sex unless they are ill, and can be denied food if they do."<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_empowerment">Education and empowerment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Human_overpopulation&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Education and empowerment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One option is to focus on education about overpopulation, <a href="/wiki/Family_planning" title="Family planning">family planning</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a> methods, and to make birth-control devices like male and <a href="/wiki/Female_condom" title="Female condom">female</a> <a href="/wiki/Condoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Condoms">condoms</a>, <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">pills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intrauterine_device" title="Intrauterine device">intrauterine devices</a> easily available.
